<div id="review_195323197" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/195323197" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/195323197?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385609340" href="/book/show/6341533-unseen-academicals">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer195323197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4387275036897207489">This is my first Discworld novel in 4 years, the last one I read being the largely mediocre "Thud!" and a below average outing for the City Watch. I tried "Making Money" a couple of years ago but it was the first Discworld I couldn't finish it was so poor. That was really it for me, I thought I'd not be returning to Pratchett again. I felt sorry for Terry hearing of his illness but his continued forays into the dubious realm of "Young Adult" fiction often yielded poor books for a bloke in his 20</span>
  <span id="freeText4387275036897207489" style="display:none">This is my first Discworld novel in 4 years, the last one I read being the largely mediocre "Thud!" and a below average outing for the City Watch. I tried "Making Money" a couple of years ago but it was the first Discworld I couldn't finish it was so poor. That was really it for me, I thought I'd not be returning to Pratchett again. I felt sorry for Terry hearing of his illness but his continued forays into the dubious realm of "Young Adult" fiction often yielded poor books for a bloke in his 20s. The bafflingly crap "Amazing Maurice" and the increasingly tedious Tiffany Aching series "Ach it's sh*t!" seemed to be where Terry was happy to stay especially as his last book was (according to a friend who is similarly disenfranchised) a poor attempt at Castaway crossed with RL Stevenson, "Nation". I think it was seeing the Librarian on the front wearing a bandana kamikaze style that brought me back. Has Terry gone back and done justice to the series that made him? Thankfully the answer is a deafening cheer from the crowd. He has.<br /> <br />The wizards' food budget is threatened if they don't observe a centuries old tradition and take part in a foot-the-ball game, and off they go. It's as simple as that, plot wise. The familiar faces are all there, Rincewind and Luggage, Death, Ridcully and Ponder Stibbons, the Librarian, CMOT Dibbler, Commander Vimes, the Patrician and Wuffles, and the glorious setting that has become a character unto itself, the city of Ankh-Morpork.<br /> <br />Though there isn't much in the way of plot, the interactions between the characters more than makes up for it. The conversations between Vetinari and Ridcully were the highlights of the book for me - two superbly realised characters verbally sparring is a rare thing in fiction today and Terry writes their scenes masterfully. If I hadn't been told that Terry had Alzeimher's or that this was the first Pratchett novel he had to dictate I wouldn't have known the difference. <br /> <br />It should be said though that the bulk of the story follows several new characters: Glenda the head of the Night Kitchen at UU, her ditzy friend Juliet, the head of the Candle Vats and talented foot-the-ball-er Trev Likely, and the mysterious goblin Nutt with a past. Glenda is your typical strong headed, independent woman character that Terry is so adept at creating while Nutt, basically the hero, is your "perceived as bad because of his appearance but salt of the earth" character that Terry's also done many times previous. Trev and Juliet are a sort of Posh'n'Becks parody. <br /> <br />Despite the book not being a revolutionary new change to Discworld, it is the type of Discworld book that I loved reading back when i was 12. Discworld has never been about plot anyway, its about the characters and the places and Terry provides a masterclass in writing both in this book. It's also a tribute to his writing ability that you don't care that Death and Vimes appear for less than a page each, or that Rincewind and Luggage are bit part players while Vetinari seems worlds apart from the character he was when we first met him, you like getting to know the new characters just as much. <br /> <br />If, like me, you just enjoy spending time in Ankh-Morpork with its eccentric citizens and brilliantly named streets, as familiar and enjoyable as seeing an old friend, you'll love this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4387275036897207489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_75532728" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75532728" />
    <a title="Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63444-seth"><img alt="Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187495654p2/63444.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75532728?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth" href="/user/show/63444-seth">Seth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=sf-f-h">sf-f-h</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=discworld">discworld</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75532728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11811667375433863463">There isn't much to say about a Discworld novel at this point.You like the series or you don't, you shouldn't start with this one (although it isn't that bad of a starter, it doesn't give a lot of background on the characters--start elsewhere), and you just want to know if it's one of the (rare) standouts, either as extra genius (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47989.Night_Watch__Discworld___27_' title='Night Watch (Discworld, #27) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Night Watch</a>) or a klunker (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34511.Monstrous_Regiment__Discworld___28_' title='Monstrous Regiment (Discworld, #28) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Monstrous Regiment</a>).<br/><br/>It's a solid Discworld book, and we seem to have precious few of those left, so cherish it. <br/><br/>For recurring characters, it </span>
  <span id="freeText11811667375433863463" style="display:none">There isn't much to say about a Discworld novel at this point.You like the series or you don't, you shouldn't start with this one (although it isn't that bad of a starter, it doesn't give a lot of background on the characters--start elsewhere), and you just want to know if it's one of the (rare) standouts, either as extra genius (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47989.Night_Watch__Discworld___27_" title="Night Watch (Discworld, #27) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Watch</a>) or a klunker (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34511.Monstrous_Regiment__Discworld___28_" title="Monstrous Regiment (Discworld, #28) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Monstrous Regiment</a>).<br /><br />It's a solid Discworld book, and we seem to have precious few of those left, so cherish it. <br /><br />For recurring characters, it revolves around Vetinari and the yahoos at the Unseen University. Lots of Mustrum Ridcully, we see Vetinari actually drunk, Lady Margolota makes an appearance, and we have passing time with Angua, the Librarian (more than usual), and Rincewind. Yes, the Luggage does drop by.<br /><br />For new characters, we get the usual lovers who probably only appear in this book, we get a classic "what is he? he's new! he plays against type!" Pratchett character (the actual star of he book), and we meet a wonderful member of the University staff, the professor of "Post Mortem Communication," which, unlike necromancy, is legal. He wears black robes, has a widow's peak and goatee, and sports the official University skull ring, so he is required to be slightly, but acceptably, evil, break rules, and undermine the staff at predictable and mostly-harmless intervals. He apologizes a lot. I loved him.<br /><br />Oh, and a *lot* of Ponder Stibbins. Which is always good. We get to see Stibbins competitive and crafty side, as well as some actual vindictiveness. He is certainly on the second- or third-tier of the Discworld's "dangerous when aroused to anger" list, the first being Vetinari and Esme Weatherwax and the second being Death, Susan Sto Lat, and Sam Vimes. Third tier is not bad.<br /><br />The plot revolves around Vetinari, for some reason, interfering in the working-class sport of football (Ankh-Morpork football is like Soccer plus Rugby plus American Football, with some cage match and fan interference thrown in). The University is forced to choose between their food budget and fielding a staff/faculty team, which means, to Ridcully's delight, the professors have to actually go outside, wear <i>trousers</i> and exercise. Which apparently does not involve picnics and smoking their pipes. Stibbins is the team coach, which leads to the expected hilarity.<br /><br />Of course, the romantic subplot, the underlying observations on class, politics, and human nature, and quietly put message about human dignity and equality are all present. Pratchett is at his best when he lets those underly, but not consume, his stories and he's as good as usual here (i.e., this <i>isn't</i> Monstrous Regiment).<br /><br />And as expected, nothing that Vetinari does is what it seems. His motives and his plans (layered several on top of each other) are brilliant as always.<br /><br />Not a "top 3" Discworld book, but well in the space of the more recent books (and far better than the early ones).</span>
  <a data-text-id="11811667375433863463" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7224313238660258570">For me, this is the best book of the series in a while, its characters are so loveable and some of the side characters like Pepe are tremendously funny. Mr Nutt especially is a wonderful character, when he starts philosiphising, for example about famous spoons, the book becomes hilarious. Terry Pratchett tells us this tale of tolerance in his own, unique</span>
  <span id="freeText7224313238660258570" style="display:none">For me, this is the best book of the series in a while, its characters are so loveable and some of the side characters like Pepe are tremendously funny. Mr Nutt especially is a wonderful character, when he starts philosiphising, for example about famous spoons, the book becomes hilarious. At first glance it looks like a book about football, but whilst reading it, I began to realise that it's really about tolerance and friendship. Terry Pratchett tells us this tale of tolerance in his own, unique style, whithout ever getting melodramatic or lecturing. "Unseen Academicals" has once again shown me why Terry Pratchett is my favourite author.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_99669082" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99669082" />
    <a title="Talulah Mankiller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3245656-talulah-mankiller"><img alt="Talulah Mankiller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285385665p2/3245656.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/99669082?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Talulah Mankiller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Talulah Mankiller" href="/user/show/3245656-talulah-mankiller">Talulah Mankiller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99669082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17429709196360913150">Dear Mr. Pratchett,<br/><br/>You have given me much joy in a cold, dreary life filled with vet visits and chemical adjustments to my brain. I was born to bleed from my vagina, expel copious amounts of snot from my nostrils (seriously, what is UP with my sinuses?), and watch GI Joe because my sister thinks terrible movies are funny. Or maybe she just finds my screams of pain amusing. ANYWAY, what I’m saying is that I suffer, I suffer mightily, but your books make things just a little bit more bearable. You</span>
  <span id="freeText17429709196360913150" style="display:none">Dear Mr. Pratchett,<br /><br />You have given me much joy in a cold, dreary life filled with vet visits and chemical adjustments to my brain. I was born to bleed from my vagina, expel copious amounts of snot from my nostrils (seriously, what is UP with my sinuses?), and watch GI Joe because my sister thinks terrible movies are funny. Or maybe she just finds my screams of pain amusing. ANYWAY, what I’m saying is that I suffer, I suffer mightily, but your books make things just a little bit more bearable. Your works are like a tiny birthday candle—one of the cheap pink-striped ones in a multi-pack—in vast cavern completely devoid of light. That is, I still can’t see shit, but at least I know that my hand exists. Or part of it, anyway. <br /><br />Since you have brought so much joy into my miserable, squalid existence, far be it from me to criticize you; instead, simply allow me to make a few humble suggestions. First, I’m sorry, but as funny as Unseen Academicals was, it definitely dragged in certain parts. You let your cleverness run away with you, I think. And second, I have some painful news for you: you know shit about men. You know that scene wherein Our Heroine, Glenda, forces her way through the crowd at the soccer match (sorry, as an American I cannot refer to soccer as “football)? Of course you do, you wrote it. In any case, Glenda is happy that she wore her most voluminous underwear so that no one can grab aholt of anything they shouldn’t be grabbing aholt of—until she realizes that no one even tried.<br /><br />All through Unseen Academicals, that’s the running joke: Glenda is fat, Glenda is dumpy, Glenda offers to kiss people and they’re all, “Err, no.” Glenda, despite being a kind, capable person and a wonderful baker, is only desirable to one person: Nutt, who is, literally, not a human being at all (we won’t get into Nutt’s provenance, since that would be spoilery). Basically, Glenda is not pretty, and this fact is played for laughs throughout the whole goddamn book.<br /><br />Now, perhaps you thought you were just making a witty contrast between Glenda and her jaw-droppingly gorgeous friend Juliet, but given your treatment of your other female characters, I somehow doubt that. You find ugly chicks—or even just mildly not-beautiful chicks—innately hilarious, and cannot seem to envision a world in which someone might find them desirable.<br /><br />I hate to say this to you, Mr. Pratchett, and it will sound seriously strange coming from a woman, but honestly? As I mentioned earlier, you just don’t understand men.<br /><br />The fact of the matter is, unless Glenda were OBSCENELY strange-looking, pretty much everyone she offered to kiss would have taken her up on it. It’s not that men will bone anything that moves (although some of them will), it’s just that you portrayed almost all of these men as having asked Juliet for a kiss before Glenda offered one. And I’m sorry, but a guy who asks for a smackeroo from one girl isn’t going to turn down one from another, just because she’s not as good-looking. Doesn’t work that way. He might not be gracious about it, he might not be complimentary about it, but he’ll take it. Why turn down a freebie?<br /><br />Also, the sad fact is that poor Glenda probably wouldn’t have made it down to the soccer pitch with her bodice unripped: thing is, men in crowds? Yeah, they’ll try to hit that. They’ll try to hit that hard. They’ll try to hit that through a restraining order and two sets of brass knuckles. They do not care what you look like. You are just an object to them. It’s like, would I rather have the pretty, delicate little tea cup? Yeah, but if someone passes me a stalwart-looking coffee mug, I’M GRABBING IT.<br /><br />So in conclusion, I would really love it if you would stop with the fat hatred and the general misogyny. Oh, and I would LOVE IT if you would get a better grip on how men actually behave. Because while I’m sure that you, personally, might be too fastidious to stick your hand up a girl like Glenda’s skirt, most men would be trying to pull off her drawers before you even realized she was there.<br /><br />Sincerely,<br /><br />Talulah “Mankiller” Mankiller</span>
  <a data-text-id="17429709196360913150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer635226054" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5929812323095033010"> I think this might even be the first Pratchett book I read since his death. And somehow it felt so fitting that most of his main characters at least visited this book. As it was fitting that the whole book ends with a big "do you think it is over?" thing. Because it sort of is, and it is sad. I personally still have a few books of his unread, but it is still sad.<br/><br/>The book itself was not one of his best books, but there were parts that made me chuckle aloud and there were parts that moved me. I a</span>
  <span id="freeText5929812323095033010" style="display:none"> I think this might even be the first Pratchett book I read since his death. And somehow it felt so fitting that most of his main characters at least visited this book. As it was fitting that the whole book ends with a big "do you think it is over?" thing. Because it sort of is, and it is sad. I personally still have a few books of his unread, but it is still sad.<br /><br />The book itself was not one of his best books, but there were parts that made me chuckle aloud and there were parts that moved me. I am not sure if it is just me or did sir Pratchett end up being a bit more of a romantic later on? I mean, his books often have this romantic sidestory to them, but for some reason this book had parts (well, okay, one part) that actually tried to make my heart burst of sheer "aww that is so cute!" feeling. I don't think that has ever happened before, not with a Pratchett book.<br /><br />Sadly the actual plot, while not bad, was a bit more fragmented than usual. I'm not sure if I'd got more out of this if I'd know more about football, or if it just was not one of his best books. The characters were as they have always been though: likeable caricatures. But somehow the plot did not quite work for me, I did not feel like reading "just one more chapter" like I usually do. And I have no idea why that is. I just know that there were parts in the book that swept me away, but quite a lot of parts that did not.<br /><br />A good book. Just not as good as I'm used to when reading Pratchett. And still somehow it all managed to make me feel a bit moist-eyed now that I've finished it. Part of it is due to the story, part of it is due to Pratchett himself. I hope he has a place in Death's garden, because aren't gardens what all Englishmen enjoy?</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_281600284" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281600284" />
    <a title="Phil McCrum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7858373-phil-mccrum"><img alt="Phil McCrum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404059922p2/7858373.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281600284?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phil McCrum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phil McCrum" href="/user/show/7858373-phil-mccrum">Phil McCrum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281600284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6825056634025274071"><br/>Wotcher!<br/><br/>"Unseen Academicals" arrives new into a series that is already well over 30 books deep. The series is referred to as the "Discworld" series. For those of you unfamiliar with Discworld, it is a mythical flat world being carried through space on the backs of four cosmically huge elephants that are, themselves, standing on the back of an even larger tortoise. This tortoise is slowly making her way through the universe carrying millions of eclectic entities with her. If you loved "The Hitchh</span>
  <span id="freeText6825056634025274071" style="display:none"><br />Wotcher!<br /><br />"Unseen Academicals" arrives new into a series that is already well over 30 books deep. The series is referred to as the "Discworld" series. For those of you unfamiliar with Discworld, it is a mythical flat world being carried through space on the backs of four cosmically huge elephants that are, themselves, standing on the back of an even larger tortoise. This tortoise is slowly making her way through the universe carrying millions of eclectic entities with her. If you loved "The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy" by Douglas Adams, you will also love the Discworld series. The biting humor is identical -- the main difference being that Hitchhiker's is more science fiction and Discworld is more fantasy. Pratchett uses the Discworld and its inhabitants to poke good-natured fun at science, religion, cultures, prejudices, sports, politics, and well... EVERYTHING.<br /><br />"Unseen Academicals" appears (from the cover) to be about the Wizard's Unseen University in the great metropolis of Ankh-Morpork being forced to engage in league "foot-the-ball" (football to the Europeans and soccer to the Americans). But it is actually about the life and 'coming of age' of a brand new character, Nutt. Nutt appears to be a goblin -- a new character type in Pratchett's mythical world of characters (I won't spoil it by saying further on who and what Nutt is). Nutt has a problem trying to decide who he is, the Unseen University has a problem trying to figure out how to participate in foot-the-ball without getting too far from the cheese carts, and quite a few non-couples are unexpectedly working their way to losing the 'non-'. Most of the action is supportive to Nutt's development. "Unseen Academicals" gives us a good dose of Lord Ventinari (the city's wonderful tyrant) and a giant dose of the scholars of Unseen University.<br /><br />As I dived into this book and started devouring it page by page, I was once again impressed -- actually astounded -- by Pratchett's ability to take mediocre events or situations and describe them in such a clever and hilarious way that you laugh out loud uncontrollably. Who would have come up with the concept of someone who is not personable and homely to boot being described as someone with "charisn'tma"? I don't think there was even one page in the whole book that didn't have some witty turn of a phrase or new and bizarre use of a regular word that made me grin like the Joker. Sadly, it's Pratchett's narrative wit that makes the Discworld series very hard to convert into movie format. Most of the wit is in the "invisible narrator's words" and less in dialogue and actions. Be that as it may, two novels ("The Color of Magic" and "Hogfather") were made into fairly long movies and were very entertaining and well-done. They would obviously be enjoyed more by diehard fans of the Discworld.<br /><br />The odd thing about Pratchett and his Discworld series is that he seems to be getting better and better. Usually, a series starts out with a bang and then peters out. So often with your run-of-the-mill fiction series you get past the fifth novel or so and you start to get more underwhelmed than anything else. Each subsequent novel seems to be even more boring than the previous one and a let-down. Not so with Pratchett. He almost seems to be gaining steam. His two recent offerings "Going Postal" and "Making Money" were some of his best works in the series and "Unseen Academicals" continues the trend. <br /><br />If you are a Discworld fan, then you probably already have read "Unseen Academicals". If you are new to the Discworld series, check out Terry Pratchett's website (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.terrypratchettbooks.com/">http://www.terrypratchettbooks.com/</a>) for a comprehensive list of his books. While reading the series in order is best, this is one series that you can jump around a bit without losing much. Check it out. <br /><br />And for Pratchett fans who are wondering... my favorite Discworld characters are Granny Weatherwax, Carrot, DEATH, Vimes, and now Nutt!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6825056634025274071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_178171442" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178171442" />
    <a title="K." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5663034-k"><img alt="K." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525318510p2/5663034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178171442?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K." class="user" itemprop="url" name="K." href="/user/show/5663034-k">K.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385609340" href="/book/show/6341533-unseen-academicals">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5663034-k?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5663034-k?shelf=own-it-hard-copy">own-it-hard-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5663034-k?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5663034-k?shelf=setting-fantasy">setting-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5663034-k?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5663034-k?shelf=audience-adult">audience-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178171442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11200896626832259602">I find this book a somewhat perplexing addition to the Discworld series. Technically, I think it's classed as a Rincewind book?? And yet Rincewind shows up for approximately two pages. <br/><br/>It's a book about football, in which the game goes from being played in a totally disorganised, stab-your-opponents-if-they-get-in-the-way fashion with a heavy wooden ball to a game with rules and specific positions and a lightweight inflatable ball. Which is fine, except that <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/833436.Jingo__Discworld___21_' title='Jingo (Discworld, #21) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jingo</a>, some sixteen books earlier in </span>
  <span id="freeText11200896626832259602" style="display:none">I find this book a somewhat perplexing addition to the Discworld series. Technically, I think it's classed as a Rincewind book?? And yet Rincewind shows up for approximately two pages. <br /><br />It's a book about football, in which the game goes from being played in a totally disorganised, stab-your-opponents-if-they-get-in-the-way fashion with a heavy wooden ball to a game with rules and specific positions and a lightweight inflatable ball. Which is fine, except that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/833436.Jingo__Discworld___21_" title="Jingo (Discworld, #21) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Jingo</a>, some sixteen books earlier in the series, features Carrot carrying around a flattened football during war, and using it - and the rules of football - to actually end the war between Klatch and Ankh-Morpork. <br /><br />When I first realised that (and yes, I know I'm being super nitpicky), I thought that maybe this book was supposed to be set somewhere prior to the events of Jingo. But no - Vimes turns up to discuss the Koom Valley peace treaty, so clearly this takes place after the events of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/62530.Thud___Discworld___34__City_Watch__7_" title="Thud! (Discworld, #34; City Watch #7) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Thud!</a>. <br /><br />So essentially, it feels like Pratchett undid canon just to write a book about sport. <br /><br />Nitpicking aside, this one dragged for me. Partly it's because it's a book about sport, and I just didn't care. But partly it's because we have a whole new cast of characters. Yes, we still have Ridcully and the rest of the wizards. But with the Dean and the Bursar having left for the new university in Quirm, the dynamic has changed. <br /><br />Instead, we're given a story that's largely from the perspective of the Unseen University's servants. Don't get me wrong - Glenda is a fabulous character, and Mister Nutt is delightful. There's a lot in here about racial stereotypes and trying to overcome them, and how if you look a certain way, you're constantly struggling to make a good impression, to prove yourself worthy, to make those in a position of privilege accept you as an actual living thing and not a monster waiting to strike. <br /><br />And all of that is excellent. I even liked the Romeo and Juliet elements of two young people from arch-rival football teams falling in love. So there's definitely good stuff in there.<br /><br />But this plods along so slowly that I never felt like I was making any progress in the story. And, for something that features The Librarian front and centre on the cover? Yeah, he's on about a dozen of its 400 pages. <br /><br />In summary? Womp. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11200896626832259602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7386534138076960981">Very fun to read, but it ended quite quickly, without the usual bang. I didn't like all of the new characters. The continuation of the story would be very interesting. All in all, it's a good Discworld book.<br/><br/>And something I've been thinking while reading the book, but not really provoked by it:<br/><br/>It seems more and more civil concepts from our world are starting their own life in Ankh Morpork. The more the Discworld gets populated by newspaper agencies, mobile communications, banks, post offices, fas</span>
  <span id="freeText7386534138076960981" style="display:none">Very fun to read, but it ended quite quickly, without the usual bang. I didn't like all of the new characters. The continuation of the story would be very interesting. All in all, it's a good Discworld book.<br /><br />And something I've been thinking while reading the book, but not really provoked by it:<br /><br />It seems more and more civil concepts from our world are starting their own life in Ankh Morpork. The more the Discworld gets populated by newspaper agencies, mobile communications, banks, post offices, fashion shows, a functioning police force... The more it starts melting away for me. Maybe losing a little bit of its brightness or spark. Maybe it's just me, but everything starting to work just as it does in our own world, even beautifully described with the usual flawless sense of humor, takes a little bit of the magic with every book. The books themselves are really good, though. I'll just leave it at that.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2505300206" />
    <a title="Andree" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2863963-andree"><img alt="Andree" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2505300206?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andree" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andree" href="/user/show/2863963-andree">Andree</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385609340" href="/book/show/6341533-unseen-academicals">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863963-andree?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2505300206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6523353720096570103">3.5 stars, but this time I rounded down, because it took me weeks to get through this.<br/><br/>Look, it's fun. Pratchett poking fun at sports (the chanting - HEH), while also highlighting some of the good things about it. Pratchett is always clever, and the character work is good. Nutt, Glenda and Trev are particularly fun, which is notable, because they're all new characters.<br/><br/>I particularly enjoyed Glenda's dynamic with Vetinari.<br/><br/>The wizards where their usual zany selves. Ponder is my favourite, but Ridic</span>
  <span id="freeText6523353720096570103" style="display:none">3.5 stars, but this time I rounded down, because it took me weeks to get through this.<br /><br />Look, it's fun. Pratchett poking fun at sports (the chanting - HEH), while also highlighting some of the good things about it. Pratchett is always clever, and the character work is good. Nutt, Glenda and Trev are particularly fun, which is notable, because they're all new characters.<br /><br />I particularly enjoyed Glenda's dynamic with Vetinari.<br /><br />The wizards where their usual zany selves. Ponder is my favourite, but Ridicully has been consistently growing on me. I enjoy that this is considered a Rincewind book, given that he's barely in it (a wise choice).<br /><br />I was somewhat tempted to round up, because anything where a particularly strict adherence to the rules essentially wins the day (complete with handing the referee a draft of the rules with the relevant one you intend to strictly adhere to) is something I really enjoy. <br /><br />But then I remember how long it took me to get into this (it picks up at the halfway point), and I stuck with my original instinct. I wonder if the problem was one of being slightly too long...<br /><br />Fun, but not a favourite.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6523353720096570103" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('52grrr6yCGP7g1eIItpPb7DPtHDFDYQXQS/tNwSfe72wEovCyqpIc8B9TgZwzl9XLWIDKlBvQSMEMA4fGDZJ/w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12929225880455103037">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br/><br/>Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, i</span>
  <span id="freeText12929225880455103037" style="display:none">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel, <i>The Color of Magic</i>, in 1983. In 1987, he turned to writing full time. <br /><br />There are over 40 books in the Discworld series, of which four are written for children. The first of these, <i>The Amazing Maurice and His Educated Rodents</i>, won the Carnegie Medal. <br /><br />A non-Discworld book, <i>Good Omens</i>, his 1990 collaboration with Neil Gaiman, has been a longtime bestseller and was reissued in hardcover by William Morrow in early 2006 (it is also available as a mass market paperback - Harper Torch, 2006 - and trade paperback - Harper Paperbacks, 2006). <br /><br />In 2008, Harper Children's published Terry's standalone non-Discworld YA novel, <i>Nation</i>. Terry published <i>Snuff</i> in October 2011. <br /><br />Regarded as one of the most significant contemporary English-language satirists, Pratchett has won numerous literary awards, was named an Officer of the British Empire (OBE) “for services to literature” in 1998, and has received honorary doctorates from the University of Warwick in 1999, the University of Portsmouth in 2001, the University of Bath in 2003, the University of Bristol in 2004, Buckinghamshire New University in 2008, the University of Dublin in 2008, Bradford University in 2009, the University of Winchester in 2009, and The Open University in 2013 for his contribution to Public Service.<br /><br />In Dec. of 2007, Pratchett disclosed that he had been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. On 18 Feb, 2009, he was knighted by Queen Elizabeth II.<br /><br />He was awarded the World Fantasy Life Achievement Award in 2010.<br /><br />Sir Terry Pratchett passed away on 12th March 2015.</span>
